For each topic, we lay out the available partitions in numeric order\\n44:  * and the consumers in lexicographic order. We then divide the number of partitions by the total number of\\n45:  * consumers to determine the number of partitions to assign to each consumer. If it does not evenly\\n46:  * divide, then the first few consumers will have one extra partition.\\n47:  *\\n48:  * <p>For example, suppose there are two consumers <code>C0</code> and <code>C1</code>, two topics <code>t0</code> and\\n49:  * <code>t1</code>, and each topic has 3 partitions, resulting in partitions <code>t0p0</code>, <code>t0p1</code>,\\n50:  * <code>t0p2</code>, <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, and <code>t1p2</code>.\\n51:  *\\n52:  * <p>The assignment will be:\\n53:  * <ul>\\n54:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0, t0p1, t1p0, t1p1]</code></li>\\n55:  * <li><code>C1: [t0p2, t1p2]</code></li>\\n56:  * </ul>\\n57:  *\\n58:  * Since the introduction of static membership, we could leverage <code>group.instance.id</code> to make the assignment behavior more sticky.\\n59:  * For the above example, after one rolling bounce, group coordinator will attempt to assign new <code>member.id</code> towards consumers,\\n60:  * for example <code>C0</code> -&gt; <code>C3</code> <code>C1</code> -&gt; <code>C2</code>.\\n61:  *\\n62:  * <p>The assignment could be completely shuffled to:\\n63:  * <ul>\\n64:  * <li><code>C3 (was C0): [t0p2, t1p2] (before was [t0p0, t0p1, t1p0, t1p1])</code>\\n65:  * <li><code>C2 (was C1): [t0p0, t0p1, t1p0, t1p1] (before was [t0p2, t1p2])</code>\\n66:  * </ul>\\n67:  *\\n68:  * The assignment change was caused by the change of <code>member.id</code> relative order, and\\n69:  * can be avoided by setting the group.instance.id.\\n70:  * Consumers will have individual instance ids <code>I1</code>, <code>I2</code>. As long as\\n71:  * 1. Number of members remain the same across generation\\n72:  * 2. Static members' identities persist across generation\\n73:  * 3. Subscription pattern doesn't change for any member\\n74:  *\\n75:  * <p>The assignment will always be:\\n76:  * <ul>\\n77:  * <li><code>I0: [t0p0, t0p1, t1p0, t1p1]</code>\\n78:  * <li><code>I1: [t0p2, t1p2]</code>\\n79:  * </ul>\\n80:  * <p>\\n81:  * Rack-aware assignment is used if both consumer and partition replica racks are available and\\n82:  * some partitions have replicas only on a subset of racks. We attempt to match consumer racks with\\n83:  * partition replica racks on a best-effort basis, prioritizing balanced assignment over rack alignment.\\n84:  * Topics with equal partition count and same set of subscribers guarantee co-partitioning by prioritizing\\n85:  * co-partitioning over rack alignment. In this case, aligning partition replicas of these topics on the\\n86:  * same racks will improve locality for consumers. For example, if partitions 0 of all topics have a replica\\n87:  * on rack 'a', partition 1 on rack 'b' etc., partition 0 of all topics can be assigned to a consumer\\n88:  * on rack 'a', partition 1 to a consumer on rack 'b' and so on.\\n89:  * <p>\\n90:  * Note that rack-aware assignment currently takes all replicas into account, including any offline replicas\\n91:  * and replicas that are not in the ISR. This is based on the assumption that these replicas are likely\\n92:  * to join the ISR relatively soon. Since consumers don't rebalance on ISR change, this avoids unnecessary\\n93:  * cross-rack traffic for long durations after replicas rejoin the ISR. In the future, we may consider\\n94:  * rebalancing when replicas are added or removed to improve consumer rack alignment.\\n95:  * </p>\\n96:  */\\n97: public class RangeAssignor extends AbstractPartitionAssignor {\\n98:     public static final String RANGE_ASSIGNOR_NAME = \\\"range\\\";\\n99:     private static final TopicPartitionComparator PARTITION_COMPARATOR = new TopicPartitionComparator();\\n100: \\n101: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kafka--0753c489@31d9200189f81283bf60e929ddc38fd68ac034e2/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/RangeAssignor.java%3FL1-100\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"4314f1a7-aebf-4d12-a0b7-4bdcdae99027","uuid":"5ec96818-a9ce-4d3c-ac19-b58bcf3d9c1b","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: /*\\n2:  * Licensed to the Apache Software Foundation (ASF) under one or more\\n3:  * contributor license agreements. We then divide the number of partitions by the total number of\\n45:  * consumers to determine the number of partitions to assign to each consumer. It\\n33:  * then proceeds to do a round robin assignment from partition to consumer. If the subscriptions of all consumer\\n34:  * instances are identical, then the partitions will be uniformly distributed. (i.e., the partition ownership counts\\n35:  * will be within a delta of exactly one across all consumers.)\\n36:  *\\n37:  * <p>For example, suppose there are two consumers <code>C0</code> and <code>C1</code>, two topics <code>t0</code> and <code>t1</code>,\\n38:  * and each topic has 3 partitions, resulting in partitions <code>t0p0</code>, <code>t0p1</code>, <code>t0p2</code>,\\n39:  * <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, and <code>t1p2</code>.\\n40:  *\\n41:  * <p>The assignment will be:\\n42:  * <ul>\\n43:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0, t0p2, t1p1]</code>\\n44:  * <li><code>C1: [t0p1, t1p0, t1p2]</code>\\n45:  * </ul>\\n46:  *\\n47:  * <p>When subscriptions differ across consumer instances, the assignment process still considers each\\n48:  * consumer instance in round robin fashion but skips over an instance if it is not subscribed to\\n49:  * the topic. Unlike the case when subscriptions are identical, this can result in imbalanced\\n50:  * assignments. For example, we have three consumers <code>C0</code>, <code>C1</code>, <code>C2</code>,\\n51:  * and three topics <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>, <code>t2</code>, with 1, 2, and 3 partitions, respectively.\\n52:  * Therefore, the partitions are <code>t0p0</code>, <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, <code>t2p0</code>, <code>t2p1</code>, <code>t2p2</code>.\\n53:  * <code>C0</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>;\\n54:  * <code>C1</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>;\\n55:  * and <code>C2</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>, <code>t2</code>.\\n56:  *\\n57:  * <p>That assignment will be:\\n58:  * <ul>\\n59:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0]</code>\\n60:  * <li><code>C1: [t1p0]</code>\\n61:  * <li><code>C2: [t1p1, t2p0, t2p1, t2p2]</code>\\n62:  * </ul>\\n63:  *\\n64:  * Since the introduction of static membership, we could leverage <code>group.instance.id</code> to make the assignment behavior more sticky.\\n65:  * For example, we have three consumers with assigned <code>member.id</code> <code>C0</code>, <code>C1</code>, <code>C2</code>,\\n66:  * two topics <code>t0</code> and <code>t1</code>, and each topic has 3 partitions, resulting in partitions <code>t0p0</code>,\\n67:  * <code>t0p1</code>, <code>t0p2</code>, <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, and <code>t1p2</code>. (i.e., the partition ownership counts\\n35:  * will be within a delta of exactly one across all consumers.)\\n36:  *\\n37:  * <p>For example, suppose there are two consumers <code>C0</code> and <code>C1</code>, two topics <code>t0</code> and <code>t1</code>,\\n38:  * and each topic has 3 partitions, resulting in partitions <code>t0p0</code>, <code>t0p1</code>, <code>t0p2</code>,\\n39:  * <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, and <code>t1p2</code>.\\n40:  *\\n41:  * <p>The assignment will be:\\n42:  * <ul>\\n43:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0, t0p2, t1p1]</code>\\n44:  * <li><code>C1: [t0p1, t1p0, t1p2]</code>\\n45:  * </ul>\\n46:  *\\n47:  * <p>When subscriptions differ across consumer instances, the assignment process still considers each\\n48:  * consumer instance in round robin fashion but skips over an instance if it is not subscribed to\\n49:  * the topic. For example, we have three consumers <code>C0</code>, <code>C1</code>, <code>C2</code>,\\n51:  * and three topics <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>, <code>t2</code>, with 1, 2, and 3 partitions, respectively.\\n52:  * Therefore, the partitions are <code>t0p0</code>, <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, <code>t2p0</code>, <code>t2p1</code>, <code>t2p2</code>.\\n53:  * <code>C0</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>;\\n54:  * <code>C1</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>;\\n55:  * and <code>C2</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>, <code>t2</code>.\\n56:  *\\n57:  * <p>That assignment will be:\\n58:  * <ul>\\n59:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0]</code>\\n60:  * <li><code>C1: [t1p0]</code>\\n61:  * <li><code>C2: [t1p1, t2p0, t2p1, t2p2]</code>\\n62:  * </ul>\\n63:  *\\n64:  * Since the introduction of static membership, we could leverage <code>group.instance.id</code> to make the assignment behavior more sticky.\\n65:  * For example, we have three consumers with assigned <code>member.id</code> <code>C0</code>, <code>C1</code>, <code>C2</code>,\\n66:  * two topics <code>t0</code> and <code>t1</code>, and each topic has 3 partitions, resulting in partitions <code>t0p0</code>,\\n67:  * <code>t0p1</code>, <code>t0p2</code>, <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, and <code>t1p2</code>. We choose to honor\\n68:  * the sorted order based on ephemeral <code>member.id</code>.\\n69:  *\\n70:  * <p>The assignment will be:\\n71:  * <ul>\\n72:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0, t1p0]</code>\\n73:  * <li><code>C1: [t0p1, t1p1]</code>\\n74:  * <li><code>C2: [t0p2, t1p2]</code>\\n75:  * </ul>\\n76:  *\\n77:  * After one rolling bounce, group coordinator will attempt to assign new <code>member.id</code> towards consumers,\\n78:  * for example <code>C0</code> -&gt; <code>C5</code> <code>C1</code> -&gt; <code>C3</code>, <code>C2</code> -&gt; <code>C4</code>.\\n79:  *\\n80:  * <p>The assignment could be completely shuffled to:\\n81:  * <ul>\\n82:  * <li><code>C3 (was C1): [t0p0, t1p0] (before was [t0p1, t1p1])</code>\\n83:  * <li><code>C4 (was C2): [t0p1, t1p1] (before was [t0p2, t1p2])</code>\\n84:  * <li><code>C5 (was C0): [t0p2, t1p2] (before was [t0p0, t1p0])</code>\\n85:  * </ul>\\n86:  *\\n87:  * This issue could be mitigated by the introduction of static membership. Consumers will have individual instance ids\\n88:  * <code>I1</code>, <code>I2</code>, <code>I3</code>. As long as\\n89:  * 1. Number of members remain the same across generation\\n90:  * 2. Static members' identities persist across generation\\n91:  * 3. Subscription pattern doesn't change for any member\\n92:  *\\n93:  * <p>The assignment will always be:\\n94:  * <ul>\\n95:  * <li><code>I0: [t0p0, t1p0]</code>\\n96:  * <li><code>I1: [t0p1, t1p1]</code>\\n97:  * <li><code>I2: [t0p2, t1p2]</code>\\n98:  * </ul>\\n99:  */\\n100: public class RoundRobinAssignor extends AbstractPartitionAssignor {\\n101: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kafka--0753c489@31d9200189f81283bf60e929ddc38fd68ac034e2/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/RoundRobinAssignor.java%3FL1-100\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"1: /*\n2:  * Licensed to the Apache Software Foundation (ASF) under one or more\n3:  * contributor license agreements. It\n33:  * then proceeds to do a round robin assignment from partition to consumer. If the subscriptions of all consumer\n34:  * instances are identical, then the partitions will be uniformly distributed. (i.e., the partition ownership counts\n35:  * will be within a delta of exactly one across all consumers.)\n36:  *\n37:  * <p>For example, suppose there are two consumers <code>C0</code> and <code>C1</code>, two topics <code>t0</code> and <code>t1</code>,\n38:  * and each topic has 3 partitions, resulting in partitions <code>t0p0</code>, <code>t0p1</code>, <code>t0p2</code>,\n39:  * <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, and <code>t1p2</code>.\n40:  *\n41:  * <p>The assignment will be:\n42:  * <ul>\n43:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0, t0p2, t1p1]</code>\n44:  * <li><code>C1: [t0p1, t1p0, t1p2]</code>\n45:  * </ul>\n46:  *\n47:  * <p>When subscriptions differ across consumer instances, the assignment process still considers each\n48:  * consumer instance in round robin fashion but skips over an instance if it is not subscribed to\n49:  * the topic. Unlike the case when subscriptions are identical, this can result in imbalanced\n50:  * assignments. For example, we have three consumers <code>C0</code>, <code>C1</code>, <code>C2</code>,\n51:  * and three topics <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>, <code>t2</code>, with 1, 2, and 3 partitions, respectively.\n52:  * Therefore, the partitions are <code>t0p0</code>, <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, <code>t2p0</code>, <code>t2p1</code>, <code>t2p2</code>.\n53:  * <code>C0</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>;\n54:  * <code>C1</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>;\n55:  * and <code>C2</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>, <code>t2</code>.\n56:  *\n57:  * <p>That assignment will be:\n58:  * <ul>\n59:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0]</code>\n60:  * <li><code>C1: [t1p0]</code>\n61:  * <li><code>C2: [t1p1, t2p0, t2p1, t2p2]</code>\n62:  * </ul>\n63:  *\n64:  * Since the introduction of static membership, we could leverage <code>group.instance.id</code> to make the assignment behavior more sticky.\n65:  * For example, we have three consumers with assigned <code>member.id</code> <code>C0</code>, <code>C1</code>, <code>C2</code>,\n66:  * two topics <code>t0</code> and <code>t1</code>, and each topic has 3 partitions, resulting in partitions <code>t0p0</code>,\n67:  * <code>t0p1</code>, <code>t0p2</code>, <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, and <code>t1p2</code>. Consumers will have individual instance ids\n88:  * <code>I1</code>, <code>I2</code>, <code>I3</code>. As long as\n89:  * 1. Number of members remain the same across generation\n90:  * 2. First, it guarantees an assignment that is as balanced as possible, meaning either:\\n38:  * <ul>\\n39:  * <li>the numbers of topic partitions assigned to consumers differ by at most one; or</li>\\n40:  * <li>each consumer that has 2+ fewer topic partitions than some other consumer cannot get any of those topic partitions transferred to it.</li>\\n41:  * </ul>\\n42:  * Second, it preserved as many existing assignment as possible when a reassignment occurs. This helps in saving some of the\\n43:  * overhead processing when topic partitions move from one consumer to another.</p>\\n44:  *\\n45:  * <p>Starting fresh it would work by distributing the partitions over consumers as evenly as possible. Each consumer is subscribed to all three topics.\\n58:  *\\n59:  * The assignment with both sticky and round robin assignors will be:\\n60:  * <ul>\\n61:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0, t1p1, t3p0]</code></li>\\n62:  * <li><code>C1: [t0p1, t2p0, t3p1]</code></li>\\n63:  * <li><code>C2: [t1p0, t2p1]</code></li>\\n64:  * </ul>\\n65:  *\\n66:  * Now, let's assume <code>C1</code> is removed and a reassignment is about to happen. First, it guarantees an assignment that is as balanced as possible, meaning either:\n38:  * <ul>\n39:  * <li>the numbers of topic partitions assigned to consumers differ by at most one; or</li>\n40:  * <li>each consumer that has 2+ fewer topic partitions than some other consumer cannot get any of those topic partitions transferred to it.</li>\n41:  * </ul>\n42:  * Second, it preserved as many existing assignment as possible when a reassignment occurs. This helps in saving some of the\n43:  * overhead processing when topic partitions move from one consumer to another.</p>\n44:  *\n45:  * <p>Starting fresh it would work by distributing the partitions over consumers as evenly as possible. Even though this may sound similar to\n46:  * how round robin assignor works, the second example below shows that it is not.\n47:  * During a reassignment it would perform the reassignment in such a way that in the new assignment\n48:  * <ol>\n49:  * <li>topic partitions are still distributed as evenly as possible, and</li>\n50:  * <li>topic partitions stay with their previously assigned consumers as much as possible.</li>\n51:  * </ol>\n52:  * Of course, the first goal above takes precedence over the second one.</p>\n53:  *\n54:  * <p><b>Example 1.</b> Suppose there are three consumers <code>C0</code>, <code>C1</code>, <code>C2</code>,\n55:  * four topics <code>t0,</code> <code>t1</code>, <code>t2</code>, <code>t3</code>, and each topic has 2 partitions,\n56:  * resulting in partitions <code>t0p0</code>, <code>t0p1</code>, <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, <code>t2p0</code>,\n57:  * <code>t2p1</code>, <code>t3p0</code>, <code>t3p1</code>. Each consumer is subscribed to all three topics.\n58:  *\n59:  * The assignment with both sticky and round robin assignors will be:\n60:  * <ul>\n61:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0, t1p1, t3p0]</code></li>\n62:  * <li><code>C1: [t0p1, t2p0, t3p1]</code></li>\n63:  * <li><code>C2: [t1p0, t2p1]</code></li>\n64:  * </ul>\n65:  *\n66:  * Now, let's assume <code>C1</code> is removed and a reassignment is about to happen. The round robin assignor would produce:\n67:  * <ul>\n68:  * <li><code>C0: [t0p0, t1p0, t2p0, t3p0]</code></li>\n69:  * <li><code>C2: [t0p1, t1p1, t2p1, t3p1]</code></li>\n70:  * </ul>\n71:  *\n72:  * while the sticky assignor would result in:\n73:  * <ul>\n74:  * <li><code>C0 [t0p0, t1p1, t3p0, t2p0]</code></li>\n75:  * <li><code>C2 [t1p0, t2p1, t0p1, t3p1]</code></li>\n76:  * </ul>\n77:  * preserving all the previous assignments (unlike the round robin assignor).\n78:  *</p>\n79:  * <p><b>Example 2.</b> There are three consumers <code>C0</code>, <code>C1</code>, <code>C2</code>,\n80:  * and three topics <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>, <code>t2</code>, with 1, 2, and 3 partitions respectively.\n81:  * Therefore, the partitions are <code>t0p0</code>, <code>t1p0</code>, <code>t1p1</code>, <code>t2p0</code>,\n82:  * <code>t2p1</code>, <code>t2p2</code>. <code>C0</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>; <code>C1</code> is subscribed to\n83:  * <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>; and <code>C2</code> is subscribed to <code>t0</code>, <code>t1</code>, <code>t2</code>.\n84:  *\n85:  * The round robin assignor would come up with the following assignment:\n86:  * <ul>\n87:  * <li><code>C0 [t0p0]</code></li>\n88:  * <li><code>C1 [t1p0]</code></li>\n89:  * <li><code>C2 [t1p1, t2p0, t2p1, t2p2]</code></li>\n90:  * </ul>\n91:  *\n92:  * which is not as balanced as the assignment suggested by sticky assignor:\n93:  * <ul>\n94:  * <li><code>C0 [t0p0]</code></li>\n95:  * <li><code>C1 [t1p0, t1p1]</code></li>\n96:  * <li><code>C2 [t2p0, t2p1, t2p2]</code></li>\n97:  * </ul>\n98:  *\n99:  * Now, if consumer <code>C0</code> is removed, these two assignors would produce the following assignments.\n100:  * Round Robin (preserves 3 partition assignments):\n101: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kafka--0753c489@31d9200189f81283bf60e929ddc38fd68ac034e2/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/StickyAssignor.java%3FL1-100"}}}
